‚Äò‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç ‡§ö ‡§™‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ’ ( ‡§ï. ‡§â. ‡•©. ‡•ß‡•ß) ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ç-‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø-‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§É ‡§∏ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ ‡•§

‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç— ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§∑‡•ã‡§≥‡§∂ ‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§£‡§É ‡§™‡•å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•Å‡§≠‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•Å‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§ó‡§§‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É ‡•§‡•§

Jamison Brereton

90 (916)
PurusaÃ£
NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa
16 verses: anu·π£·π≠ubh, except tri·π£·π≠ubh 16

This is one of the best-known and most influential hymns of the RÃ•gveda. Its central symbol is the p√∫ru·π£a, the ‚Äúman,‚Äù ‚Äúperson,‚Äù or ‚Äúhuman being.‚Äù

On the surface, this hymn tells of the sacrifice of a giant man, from whose parts the world was created. The theme of creation through a cosmic sacrifice is widespread, but this hymn is not simply the retelling of an ancient tradition. The p√∫ru·π£a here serves as a symbol of the sacrifice itself, which especially in the middle Vedic tradition is a locus of creative power. The p√∫ru·π£a is thus similar to the later divine figure PrajƒÅpati, who in the BrƒÅhma·πáas personifies the sacrifice.

Indeed this late hymn represents a verse commentary on the sacrifice that prefigures the prose commentaries of the BrƒÅhma·πáas.+++(5)+++

This hymn is also notable because it is the only RÃ•gvedic mention of the four var·πáas, the hierarchical division of the social order that forms the theoretical basis for the caste system. One reason that the hymn may have been included in the RÃ•gveda to provide a RÃ•gvedic charter for such a division of society.

The identity of the Man and the sacrifice is established in the opening verse, for his thousand heads, eyes, and feet recall Agni, the sacrificial fire, and his macro cosmic equivalent, the Sun (cf. Brown 1931: 109‚Äì10). The Man comprehends the earth and extends beyond it. As Mus (1968: 549) has pointed out, the ‚Äúten-fingers‚Äô breadth‚Äù by which he exceeds the world measures from the Man‚Äôs hairline to his mouth.+++(4)+++ The Man‚Äôs mouth represents speech, which marks the boundary between the imperceptible world of thought and the perceptible world created by speech.+++(5)+++

His mouth is also associated with eating, and in verse 2 the Man rises beyond the world ‚Äúthrough food,‚Äù that is, by making the world his food. In later texts the ‚Äúeater‚Äù is the master and the ‚Äúeaten‚Äù is one who benefits the master (cf. Rau 1957: 34‚Äì35). The image may therefore reflect the Man‚Äôs dominance over the world.+++(4)+++ See also X.125, in which the mouth is similarly both the locus of speech and the locus of eating.

This theme of dominance or rule continues also in verse 5 in the mutual generation of the Man and the VirƒÅj. The word virƒÅÃÅj can mean either ‚Äúbrilliant‚Äù or ‚Äúruling, rule.‚Äù The latter is the more likely sense here, and therefore this word connects the hymn to Vedic ideals of the king, who in his consecration encompasses the world in a similar way that the Man does here (Proferes 2007: esp. ch. 3). Since the term virƒÅÃÅj is grammatically feminine, it complements p√∫ru·π£a, which is masculine in grammar and connotation.+++(4)+++

The Man is the ‚Äúoffering‚Äù (vs. 6), the sacrificial victim (vs. 15), but more especially he is the sacrifice itself that the gods, the SƒÅdhyas, and seers performed (vs. 7). Through this sacrifice the elements of subsequent sacrifices emerged: the ‚Äúclotted-mixture‚Äù (vs. 8), the verses, chants, meters, and sacrificial formulae (vs. 9), and the sacrificial animals (vs. 10). The parts of the sacrifice also became the three upper var·πáas (vss. 11‚Äì12): his mouth the brahmins, masters of knowledge and speech; his arms the rulers, the possessors of power; and his thighs the freemen or clansmen, who are the productive support of society. These three classes form parts of the sacrifice because they can participate in the sacrifice. The ≈õ≈´dras or ‚Äúservants‚Äù are not part of the sacrifice but rather emerge from the feet of the Man, a symbol of their low social status and their exclusion from the sacrifice. Finally, the elements of the cosmos and gods themselves come forth from the sacrifice (vss. 13‚Äì14). This primeval sacrifice thus establishes the ‚Äúfirst foundations‚Äù for the performance of the sacrifice or even for the ritual, social, divine, and visible worlds more generally (vs. 16ab), and it creates access to heaven (16cd).

The identity of the SƒÅdhyas (in vss. 7 and 16) is not clear. Their name means ‚Äúthose to be brought to success,‚Äù and they appear to be ancient sacrificers whose proper ritual performance has ‚Äúperfected‚Äù them and who have attained god-like status or the status of gods.+++(4)+++

‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•ã ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§°‡§æ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É ‡§∏‡•ã‡§Ω‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§™‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§ø ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡•§ ‡§∏‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ó‡•ã‡§≤‡§ï‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¶‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§¶‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§™‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§¨‡§π‡§ø‡§∞‡§™‡§ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ã ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúPuru·π£a, who has a thousand heads, a thousand eyes, a thousand feet, investing the earth in alldirections, exceeds (it by a space) measuring ten fingers.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Puru·π£a = embodied spirit; or, virƒÅj, the aggregate ofall living beings, spirit embodied in the egg of BrahmƒÅ, i.e., the universal spirit animating all creation. Thousand= an infinite number; the human soul, extending from the navel, takes upits abode in the heart or the humanbreast; the supreme soul, having animated the universe, is present in man, either in a minute form or of definitedimensions

Jamison Brereton

The Man has a thousand heads, a thousand eyes, and a thousand feet. Having covered the earth on all sides, he extended ten fingers‚Äô breadth beyond.

Griffith

A THOUSAND heads hath Purusa, a thousand eyes, a thousand feet.
On every side pervading earth he fills a space ten fingers wide.

Geldner

Tausendk√∂pfig, tausend√§ugig, tausendf√º√üig ist Purusa; er bedeckte vollst√§ndig die Erde und erhob sich zehn Finger hoch dar√ºber.

Grassmann

Tausendk√∂pfig war der Urmensch, tausend√§ugig, tausendf√ºssig; nachdem er die Erde von allen Seiten √ºberdeckt hatte, ragte er noch zehn Finger lang dar√ºber hinaus.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúPuru·π£a is verily all this (visible world), all that is, and all that is to be; he is also the lord of immortality;for he mounts beyond (his own condition) for the food (of living beings).‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

He mounts: li·π≠, since he rises beyond byfood; or, he is that which grows by nourishment; or, that which expands by nourshment;

Annena = prƒÅ·πáinƒÅmbhoghenƒÅnnena nimittabh≈´tena; “inasmuch as he assumes the condition of the world in order that sentientbeings may enjoy the fruit of their acts (prƒÅ·πáinƒÅm karmaphalabhogƒÅya), that is not his true nature”: thesupreme spirit, which in its own state is inert and undiscernible, becomes the visible world, that living beings mayreap the fruit of their acts since they acquire mok·π£a or final liberation, the supreme spirit is the lord of immortality;

Anna = matter (Mu·πá·∏çaka Upani·π£ad 1.8

Jamison Brereton

The Man alone is this whole (world): what has come into being and what is to be.
Moreover, he is master of immortality when he climbs beyond (this
world) through food.

Griffith

This Purusa is all that yet hath been and all that is to be;
The Lord of Immortality which waxes greater still by food.

Geldner

Purusa allein ist diese ganze Welt, die vergangene und die zuk√ºnftige, und er ist der Herr √ºber die Unsterblichkeit und auch √ºber das, was durch Speise noch weiter w√§chst.

Grassmann

Der Urmensch ist alles dies, was gewesen ist, und was sein wird; er gebietet √ºber das Unsterbliche, was durch Speise [Opferspeise] noch h√∂her w√§chst [herrlicher wird].

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúSuch is his greatness; and Puru·π£a is greater even than this; all beings are one-fourth of him; his otherthree-fourths, (being) immortal, (abide) in heaven.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Such is his greatness: i.e., the greatness of Puru·π£a is as vastas the world of past, present, and future beings

Jamison Brereton

So much is his greatness, but the Man is more than this:
a quarter of him is all living beings; three quarters are the immortal in heaven.

Griffith

So mighty is his greatness; yea, greater than this is Purusa.
All creatures are one-fourth of him, three-fourths eternal life in heaven.

Geldner

Solches ist seine Gr√∂√üe und noch gewaltiger als dies ist Purusa. Ein Viertel von ihm sind alle Gesch√∂pfe, drei Viertel von ihm ist das Unsterbliche im Himmel.

Grassmann

So bedeutend ist seine Gr√∂sse, und noch gr√∂sser ist der Urmensch; ein Viertel von ihm sind alle Gesch√∂pfe, und drei Viertel von ihm die Welt der Unsterblichen im Himmel.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThree-fourths of Puru·π£a ascended; the other fourth that remained in this world proceeds repeatedly,and, diversified in various forms, went to all animate and inanimate creation.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Proceeds repeatedly: either inindividuals by death and birth, or in the world by its temporary dissolution and renovation;

SƒÅ≈õanƒÅna≈õane =those who eat and those who do not; i.e., the two classes of created things, those capable of enjoyment etc., asgods, men, animals, or incapable thereof, as mountains and rivers – conscious and unconscious creation

Jamison Brereton

With his three quarters the Man went upward, but a quarter of him came to be here again.
From there he strode out in different directions toward what eats and what does not eat.

Griffith

With three-fourths Purusa went up: onefourth of him again was here.
Thence he strode out to every side over what cats not and what cats.

Geldner

Zu drei Vierteln stieg Purusa empor, ein Viertel von ihm verj√ºngte sich hienieden. Von dem aus ging er nach allen Seiten auseinander und erstreckte sich √ºber alles was Speise i√üt und was nicht i√üt.

Grassmann

Mit drei Vierteln ging der Urmensch oben hinauf, ein Viertel von ihm erschien wieder hier, dann verbreitete er sich √ºber alles, was Speise geniesst, und was sie nicht geniesst.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É) ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§µ‡§π ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§§‡•Ä‡§® ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ (‡§ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§É-‡§â‡§¶‡•à‡§§‡•ç) ‡§®‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ä‡§™‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•à (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§É) ‡§á‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞ (‡§á‡§π ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É-‡§Ö‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§á‡§ß‡§∞ ‡§®‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É-‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à (‡§§‡§§‡§É) ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§§‡•ç(‡§∏‡§æ‡§∂‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§∂‡§®‡•á-‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø) ‡§≠‡•ã‡§ó‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§ø‡§§-‡§≠‡•ã‡§ó‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§≠‡•ã‡§ó‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§® ‡§≠‡•ã‡§ó‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§ú‡•ú ‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø (‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç-‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•ã‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡•™‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúFrom him was born VirƒÅt. and from VirƒÅt. Puru·π£a;
he, as soon as born, became manifested,
and afterwards (created) the earth (and) then corporeal forms.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

From him: tasmƒÅt = from that one-fourth; or, from the spirit who by his delusion created the body of VirƒÅ·π≠, i.e., the egg of Brahma and entered into it in the form oflife

Jamison Brereton

From him the VirƒÅj was born; from the VirƒÅj the Man.
Upon his birth, he reached beyond the earth from behind and also from in front.

Griffith

From him Viraj was born;
again Purusa from Viraj was born.
As soon as he was born he spread eastward and westward o’er the earth.

Geldner

Aus ihm ward die Viraj geboren, aus der Viraj der Purusa. Geboren ragte er hinten und vorn √ºber die Erde hinaus.

Grassmann

Von ihm ward der Allherrscher [vir√¢j] geboren und vom Allherrscher der Urmensch. Als er geboren war √ºberragte er die Erde von hinten und von vorn.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhen the gods performed the sacrifice with Puru·π£a as the offering, then Spring was its ghƒ´, Summerthe fuel, and Autumn the oblation.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Apparently a reference to an imaginary sacrifice (mƒÅnasam)

Jamison Brereton

When, with the Man as the offering, the gods extended the sacrifice, spring was its melted butter, summer its firewood, autumn its offering.

Griffith

When Gods prepared the sacrifice with Purusa as their offering,
Its oil was spring, the holy gift was autumn; summer was the wood.

Geldner

Als die G√∂tter mit Purusa das Opfer vollzogen, da war der Fr√ºhling dessen Schmelzbutter, der Sommer das Brennholz, der Herbst die Opfergabe.

Grassmann

Als die G√∂tter ihr Opfer mit dem Urmenschen als dem Opfertrank kunstvoll bereiteten, da war der Fr√ºhling sein Opferschmalz, der Sommer sein Brennholz, der Herbst sein Opfertrank.

‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•á‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡•á ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§ï‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡§Ø‡§É (‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡•á‡§£ ‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§Ö‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§§) ‡§®‡§ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§®‡•ç (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§è‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§µ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É-‡§Ü‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§Ü‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç-‡§µ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§ñ‡§≤‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§∑‡§ß‡§Ø ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§Ω‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¨‡•ã‡§ß‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§É-‡§á‡§ß‡•ç‡§Æ‡§É) ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç-‡§Ø‡§§‡•ã ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡•á ‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á (‡§∂‡§∞‡§§‡•ç-‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§É) ‡§∂‡§∞‡§¶‡•É‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç-‡§∂‡§∞‡§¶‡§ø ‡§µ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•É‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ-‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡§∞‡§§‡•ç ‡••‡•¨‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúSeven were the enclosures of the sacrifice, thrice seven logs of fuel were prepared, when the gods,celebrating the rite, bound Puru·π£a as the victim.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Seven enclosures: sapta paridhaya·∏• = seven metres, ga-yatrƒ´etc., and also as meaning the shallow trenches, three of which were dug round the ƒÄhavanƒ´ya fireplace, threeround the northern altar, and the seven ideally dug round the sun to keep off evil spirits. paridhaya·∏• = moats; or,the seven oceans; thrice seven pieces of fuel = twelve months of the year, the five seasons, the three worlds andthe sun; or the three classes of seven metres eaach: Taittirƒ´ya Sa·πÉhitƒÅ 5.1.10.3

Jamison Brereton

Its enclosing sticks were seven; the kindling sticks were made three times seven,
when the gods, extending the sacrifice, bound the Man as the
(sacrificial) animal.

Griffith

Seven fencing-sticks had he, thrice seven layers of fuel were prepared,
When the Gods, offering sacrifice, bound, as their victim, Purusa.

Geldner

Ihn besprengten, weihten sie als Opfer auf dem Barhis, den am Anfang geborenen Purusa. Diesen brachten die G√∂tter, die Sadhya¬¥s und die Rishi¬¥s sich zum Opfer.

Grassmann

Diesen zuerst geborenen Urmenschen benetzten sie als ihr Opfer auf der heiligen Streu; durch ihn opferten die G√∂tter die SƒÅdhja’s [die zu gewinnenden] und die Rischi’s.

English translation:

‚ÄúThey immolated as the victim upon the sacred grass - Puru·π£a, born before (creation); with him the deities who were SƒÅdhyas and those who were ·πö·π£is sacrificed.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

SƒÅdhya = competent to create, i.e.,PrajƒÅpati and the rest

Jamison Brereton

On the ritual grass they consecrated that sacrifice, the Man, born at the beginning.
With him the gods sacrificed, (also) the SƒÅdhyas and those who
were seers.

Griffith

They balmed as victim on the grass Purusa born in earliest time.
With him the Deities and all Sadhyas and Rsis sacrificed.

Geldner

Aus diesem vollst√§ndig geopferten Opfer wurde das Opferschmalz gewonnen. Das machte er zu den in der Luft, im Wald und im Dorfe lebenden Tieren.

Aus diesem vollst√§ndig dargebrachten Opfer sammelte sich das triefende Opferschmalz; er schuf die Thiere der Luft, der W√§lder und der D√∂rfer [die Hausthiere].

‡§∂‡•å‡§®‡§ï-‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ-‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç

‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§π‡•Å‡§§‡§∏‡•ç ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•ç - ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•É‡§∑‡§¶‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§¶‡§ß‡§ø‡§Æ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•É‡§∑‡§¶‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡•§ ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç ‡•§

‡§Ö‡§• ‡§§‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•É‡§∑‡§¶‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§§‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§É ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø-‡§ñ‡•Å‡§∞–‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡§¶-‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø-‡§∏‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∏‡•É‡§™-‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø-‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ï‡§ü-‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§-‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§è‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∂‡•Ç‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ç ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∞‡•á ‡•§ ‘‡§§‡§ø‡§°‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§ø‡§°‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø’ (‡§™‡§æ.‡§∏‡•Ç.) ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ù‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§É ‡•§

‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ö ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§ó‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡§≠‡•ã‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§è‡§µ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∂‡•Ç‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∞‡•á ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡•§ ‘‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§≠‡§µ‡§É’ (‡§™‡§æ.‡§∏‡•Ç.‡•™-‡•©-‡•´‡•©) ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§£‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§É ‡•§ ‘‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§ñ‡•ã ’ (‡§™‡§æ.‡§∏‡•Ç.‡•™-‡•®-‡•Ø‡•™) ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∂‡•à‡§∑‡§ø‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§û‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§É ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§∂‡•Å‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§£‡•á ‡§§‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§£‡•á ‡§Ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§É ‡•§

‡§™‡§∂‡•Ç‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§§‡•à‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‘‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§µ‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§π ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§É ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§ñ‡§≤‡•Å ‡§µ‡•à ‡§™‡§∂‡§µ‡§É ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§µ ‡§è‡§µ‡•à‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§¶‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡§ø’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø (‡§§‡•à.‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ.‡•©-‡•®-‡•ß-‡•™) ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡§ß‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ‡•§

‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á - ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§π‡•Å‡§§‡§∏‡•ç ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§Ç ‡§™‡•É‡§∑‡§¶‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç - ‡§™‡•É‡§∑‡§§‡•ç = ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç, ‡§¨‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§

English translation:

‚ÄúFrom that victim, in whom the universal oblation was offered, the mixture of curds and butter was produced, (then) he made those animals over whom VƒÅyu presides, those that are wild, and those that aretame.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

VƒÅyu: vƒÅyuvyan: Taittirƒ´ya BrƒÅhma·πáa 3.2.1.3; or, who are governed by instrumental nct

Jamison Brereton

From that sacrifice, when it was offered in full, the clotted-butter mixture was collected.
It [=the sacrifice] was made into the animals: those of the air (and both) those that belong to the wilderness and those that belong to the village.

Griffith

From that great general sacrifice the dripping fat was gathered up.
He formed the creatures of-the air, and animals both wild and tame.

Geldner

Aus diesem vollst√§ndig geopferten Opfer entstanden die Verse und Sangesweisen, aus ihm entstanden die Metren, aus ihm entstand der Opferspruch.

Grassmann

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúFrom that victim, in whom the universal oblation was offered, the ·πõccas and sƒÅmans were produced;from him the metres were born; from him the yajus. was born.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

TasmƒÅd yaj√±ƒÅt = from that simple portionsurnamed the universal sacrifice, sarvah≈´ta·∏•, meaning Puru·π£a as the world;

Yaj√±a = yaj√±asƒÅdhana, thematerial of sacrifice, i.e., the victim. Puru·π£a is the spiritual cause and effect of material creation; it is from him,not from the ideal or real sacrifice, that all things originate\

Jamison Brereton

From this sacrifice, when it was offered in full, the verses and chants were born.
Meters were born from it. The sacrificial formula‚Äîfrom it that was born.

Griffith

From that great general sacrifice Rcas and Sama-hymns were born:
Therefrom were spells and charms produced; the Yajus had its birth from it.

Geldner

Aus ihm entstanden die Rosse und alle Tiere mit doppelter Zahnreihe, aus ihm entstanden die Rinder, aus ihm sind die Ziegen und Schafe entstanden.

Grassmann

Aus ihm entsprangen die Rosse und alle mit zwei Zahnreihen versehenen Thiere; aus ihm entsprangen die Rinder, aus ihm die Ziegen und Schafe.

Wilson
Jamison Brereton

From it horses were born and whatever animals have teeth in both jaws. Cows were born from it. From it were born goats and sheep.

Griffith

From it were horses born, from it all cattle with two rows of teeth:
From it were generated kine, from it the goats and sheep were born.

Grassmann

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúWhen they immolated Puru·π£a, into how many portions did they divide him? What was his mouth called, what his arms, what his thighs, what were his feet called?‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

When they apportioned the Man, into how many parts did they arrange him?
What was his mouth? What his two arms? What are said to be his two thighs, his two feet?

Griffith

When they divided Purusa how many portions did they make?
What do they call his mouth, his arms? What do they call his thighs and feet?

Geldner

Sein Mund ward zum Brahmanen, seine beiden Arme wurden zum Rajanya gemacht, seine beiden Schenkel zum Vaisya, aus seinen F√º√üen entstand der Sudra.

Sein Mund ward zum Brahmanen, seine Arme zum RƒÅdschanja, seine Schenkel zum Vai√ßja, aus seinen F√ºssen entsprang der √á≈´dra.

‡§á‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§™‡§§‡•á‡§É ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§É ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§ ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§æ‡§¶‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É ‡§∏‡§É ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Ç ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§É ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§™‡§§‡•á‡§É ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡•å ‡§ä‡§∞‡•Ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§É ‡§µ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ä‡§∞‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•Ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§∂‡•Ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§ ‡•§ ‡§á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•Å‡§É‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡•á ‚Äò‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§’ (‡§§‡•à. ‡§∏‡§Ç. ‡•≠. ‡•ß. ‡•ß. ‡•™ ) ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•á ‡§â‡§≠‡•á ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§§‡§Ø‡•à‡§µ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡••

English translation:

‚ÄúHis mouth became the BrƒÅhma·πáa, his arms became the RƒÅjanya, his thighs became the Vai≈õya;the ≈ö≈´dra was born from his feet.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

The BrƒÅhma·πáa was his mouth; the RƒÅjanya was made his arms; thatwhich was the Vai≈õya was his thighs; the S’≈™dra sprang from his feet: Muir, Sanskrit Texts, vol. 1, p.9

Jamison Brereton

The brahmin was his mouth. The ruler was made his two arms.
As to his thighs‚Äîthat is what the freeman was. From his two feet the servant was born.

Griffith

The Brahman was his mouth, of both his arms was the Rajanya made.
His thighs became the Vaisya, from his feet the Sudra was produced.

Geldner

Der Mond ist aus seinem Geist entstanden, die Sonne entstand aus seinem Auge; aus seinem Munde Indra und Agni, aus seinem Aushauch entstand der Wind.

Grassmann

Aus seinem Geiste entsprang der Mond, aus seinem Auge die Sonne, aus seinem Munde Indra und Agni, aus seinem Athem der Wind.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThe moon was born from his mind; the sun was born from his eye; Indra and Agni were born from his mouth, VƒÅyu from his breath.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Or, air and breath proceeded from his ear and fire rose from his mouth: Yaju·π£

Jamison Brereton

The moon was born from his mind. From his eye the sun was born. From his mouth Indra and Agni, from his breath VƒÅyu was born.

Griffith

The Moon was gendered from his mind, and from his eye the Sun had birth;
Indra and Agni from his mouth were born, and Vayu from his breath.

Geldner

Aus dem Nabel ward der Luftraum, aus dem Haupte ging der Himmel hervor, aus den F√º√üen die Erde, aus dem Ohre die Weltgegenden. So regelten sie die Welten.

Grassmann

Aus seinem Nabel ward die Luft, aus seinem Kopf entstand der Himmel, aus seinen F√ºssen die Erde, aus seinem Ohr die Weltgegenden; so bildeten sie die Welten.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúFrom his navel came the firmament, from his head the heaven was produced, the earth from his feet,the quarters of space from his ear, so they constituted the world.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

From his navel was the midspace. From his head the heaven developed. From his two feet the earth, and the directions from his ear. Thus they arranged the worlds.

Griffith

Forth from his navel came mid-air the sky was fashioned from his head
Earth from his feet, and from his car the regions. Thus they formed the worlds.

Geldner

Sieben waren seine Umlegh√∂lzer, dreimal sieben Brennh√∂lzer wurden gemacht, als die G√∂tter das Opfer vollzogen und Purusa als Opfertier anbanden.

Grassmann

Sieben Umschlussh√∂lzer waren ihm, dreimal sieben Schichten des Brennholzes wurden bereitet, als die G√∂tter, das Opfer ausr√ºstend, den Urmenschen als Opferthier anbanden.

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúBy sacrifice the gods worshipped (him who is also) the sacrifice; those were the first duties. Those great ones became partakers of the heaven where the ancient deities the SƒÅdhyas abide. ‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

With the sacrifice the gods performed the sacrifice for themselves: these were the first foundations.
These, its greatness, accompanied (it) to heaven‚Äôs vault,
where the ancient SƒÅdhyas and the gods are.

Whitney

Translation

  1. By the sacrifice the gods sacrificed to the sacrifice; those were
    the first ordinances (dh√°rman); those greatnesses attach themselves to
    (sac) the firmament, where are the ancient (p≈´ÃÅrva) perfectible
    (sƒÅdhy√°) gods.

Notes
The verse is RV. i. 164. 50,* found also in VS. (xxxi. 16), TS. (iii. 5. 11‚Åµ), TA. (iii. 12. 7), MS. (iv. 10. 3), CB. (x. 2. 2¬≥, with
comment); ‚åäKa·π≠ha-hss., p. 83;‚åã the only variant is sacante for
sacanta in TS.TA.

“This passage and vii. 79. 2 cast light upon the idea of sƒÅdhya; there are two kinds of gods:

  • those with Indra at their head and
  • the sƒÅdhya ’they who are to be won’ (sƒÅdhya ‘what is to be
    brought into order, under control, or into comprehension’).

They are thus the unknown, conceived as preceding the known. Later they are worked into the ordinary classification of Vasus, Rudras, etc.; and what was formerly a serious religious problem, a hierarchy conceived as possible before the now-accepted gods (something like the pre-Olympians with the Greeks), has become an empty name.”

R. *‚åäAlso RV. x. 90. 16.‚åã

Griffith

Gods, sacrificing, sacrificed the victim these were the earliest holy ordinances.
The Mighty Ones attained the height of heaven, there where the Sidhyas, Gods of old, are dwelling.

Griffith - AV

The Gods adored the Sacrifice with worship: these were the statutes of primeval ages. Those mighty ones attained the cope of heaven, there where the Sadhyas, Gods of old, are dwelling.

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É)‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§ú‡§® (‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡•á‡§® ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§§) ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø-‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç-‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç (‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø) ‡§µ‡•á ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§® ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§ø-‡§Ü‡§∏‡§®‡•ç)-‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•á-‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç (‡§§‡•á ‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É) ‡§µ‡•á ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§è‡§Å (‡§π ‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á) ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ-‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞) ‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§Å (‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§è‡§Å (‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡•¨‡••

‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ ‡§ã‡§∑‡§ø‡§ú‡§® ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ-‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡•á ‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§™‡§π‡•Å‡§Å‡§ö‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡••‡•ß‡•¨‡••

‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É) ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É (‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡•á‡§®-‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Æ‡•ç-‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§§) ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡•á‡§® ‡§Ø‡§ú‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø (‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§ø-‡§Ü‡§∏‡§®‡•ç) ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§•‡§Æ‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ñ‡§≤‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç (‡§§‡•á ‡§π ‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É ‡§∏‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§) ‡§§‡•á ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á (‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡••‡•ß‡•¨‡••
